</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Branch of mathematics</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Number_Theory_(book)" class="mw-redirect" title="Number Theory (book)">Number Theory (book)</a> or <a href="/wiki/Numerology" title="Numerology">Numerology</a>.</div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:A_150x150_Ulam_spiral_of_dots_with_varying_widths_(emphasis_primes).svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0b/A_150x150_Ulam_spiral_of_dots_with_varying_widths_%28emphasis_primes%29.svg/250px-A_150x150_Ulam_spiral_of_dots_with_varying_widths_%28emphasis_primes%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="250" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0b/A_150x150_Ulam_spiral_of_dots_with_varying_widths_%28emphasis_primes%29.svg/500px-A_150x150_Ulam_spiral_of_dots_with_varying_widths_%28emphasis_primes%29.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="912" data-file-height="912" /></a><figcaption>The distribution of <a href="/wiki/Prime_number" title="Prime number">prime numbers</a>, a central point of study in number theory, illustrated by an <a href="/wiki/Ulam_spiral" title="Ulam spiral">Ulam spiral</a>. It shows the conditional <a href="/wiki/Independence_(probability_theory)" title="Independence (probability theory)">independence</a> between being prime and being a value of certain quadratic polynomials.</figcaption></figure>

rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333133064" /><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Math_topics_sidebar" title="Template:Math topics sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Math_topics_sidebar" title="Template talk:Math topics <p><b>Number theory</b> is a branch of <a href="/wiki/Pure_mathematics" title="Pure mathematics">pure mathematics</a> devoted primarily to the study of the <a href="/wiki/Integer" title="Integer">integers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic_function" title="Arithmetic function">arithmetic functions</a>. Number theorists study <a href="/wiki/Prime_number" title="Prime number">prime numbers</a> as well as the properties of <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_object" title="Mathematical object">mathematical objects</a> constructed from integers (for example, <a href="/wiki/Rational_number" title="Rational number">rational numbers</a>), or defined as generalizations of the integers (for example, <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_integer" title="Algebraic integer">algebraic integers</a>). 
</p><p>Integers can be considered either in themselves or as solutions to equations (<a href="/wiki/Diophantine_geometry" title="Diophantine geometry">Diophantine geometry</a>). Questions in number theory can often be understood through the study of <a href="/wiki/Complex_analysis" title="Complex analysis">analytical</a> objects, such as the <a href="/wiki/Riemann_zeta_function" title="Riemann zeta function">Riemann zeta function</a>, that encode properties of the integers, primes or other number-theoretic objects in some fashion (<a href="/wiki/Analytic_number_theory" title="Analytic number theory">analytic number theory</a>). One may also study <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real numbers</a> in relation to rational numbers, as for instance how irrational numbers can be approximated by fractions (<a href="/wiki/Diophantine_approximation" title="Diophantine approximation">Diophantine approximation</a>).
</p><p>Number theory is one of the oldest branches of mathematics alongside geometry. One quirk of number theory is that it deals with statements that are simple to understand but are very difficult to solve. Examples of this are <a href="/wiki/Fermat%27s_Last_Theorem" title="Fermat&#39;s Last Theorem">Fermat's Last Theorem</a>, which was proved 358 years after the original formulation, and <a href="/wiki/Goldbach%27s_conjecture" title="Goldbach&#39;s conjecture">Goldbach's conjecture</a>, which remains unsolved since the 18th century. German mathematician <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a> (1777–1855) once remarked, "Mathematics is the queen of the sciences—and number theory is the queen of mathematics."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTELong19721&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong19721-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It was regarded as the epitome of pure mathematics, with no applications outside mathematics, until the 1970s, when prime numbers became the basis for the creation of <a href="/wiki/Public-key_cryptography" title="Public-key cryptography">public-key cryptography</a> algorithms, such as the <a href="/wiki/RSA_cryptosystem" title="RSA cryptosystem">RSA cryptosystem</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Definition">Definition</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Number_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Number theory is the branch of mathematics that studies <a href="/wiki/Integer" title="Integer">integers</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Property_(mathematics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Property (mathematics)">properties</a> and relations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:7&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The integers comprise a <a href="/wiki/Set_(mathematics)" title="Set (mathematics)">set</a> that extends the set of <a href="/wiki/Natural_number" title="Natural number">natural numbers</a> <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle \{1,2,3,\dots \}}">

  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d8486c1baa9129ff0c36f7692ad6901f457b041f" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:17.062ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle \{-1,-2,-3,\dots \}}"></span>. Number theorists study <a href="/wiki/Prime_number" title="Prime number">prime numbers</a> as well as the properties of <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_object" title="Mathematical object">mathematical objects</a> constructed from integers (for example, <a href="/wiki/Rational_number" title="Rational number">rational numbers</a>), or defined as generalizations of the integers (for example, <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_integer" title="Algebraic integer">algebraic integers</a>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:5&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Number theory is closely related to arithmetic and some authors use the terms as synonyms.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the word "arithmetic" is used today to mean the study of numerical operations and extends to the <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real numbers</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In a more specific sense, number theory is restricted to the study of integers and focuses on their properties and relationships.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Traditionally, it is known as higher arithmetic.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By the early twentieth century, the term <i>number theory</i> had been widely adopted.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The term number means whole numbers, which refers to either the natural numbers or the integers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:6&#95;11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Elementary_number_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Elementary number theory">Elementary number theory</a> studies aspects of integers that can be investigated using elementary methods such as <a href="/wiki/Elementary_proof" title="Elementary proof">elementary proofs</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Analytic_number_theory" title="Analytic number theory">Analytic number theory</a>, by contrast, relies on <a href="/wiki/Complex_numbers" class="mw-redirect" title="Complex numbers">complex numbers</a> and techniques from analysis and calculus.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_number_theory" title="Algebraic number theory">Algebraic number theory</a> employs <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_structures" class="mw-redirect" title="Algebraic structures">algebraic structures</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Field_(mathematics)" title="Field (mathematics)">fields</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ring_(mathematics)" title="Ring (mathematics)">rings</a> to analyze the properties of and relations between numbers. <a href="/wiki/Geometric_number_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Geometric number theory">Geometric number theory</a> uses concepts from geometry to study numbers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Further branches of number theory are <a href="/wiki/Probabilistic_number_theory" title="Probabilistic number theory">probabilistic number theory</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Combinatorial_number_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Combinatorial number theory">combinatorial number theory</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Computational_number_theory" title="Computational number theory">computational number theory</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and applied number theory, which examines the application of number theory to science and technology.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Number_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Plimpton_322.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Babylonian tablet listing Pythagorean triples." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Plimpton_322.jpg/250px-Plimpton_322.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="173" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Plimpton_322.jpg/500px-Plimpton_322.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1246" data-file-height="863" /></a><figcaption>The Babylonians demonstrated an early understanding of Pythagorean triples.</figcaption></figure>
<p>In recorded history, knowledge of numbers existed in the ancient civilisations of Mesopotamia, Egypt, China, and India.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The earliest historical find of an arithmetical nature is the <a href="/wiki/Plimpton_322" title="Plimpton 322">Plimpton 322</a>, dated c.&#160;1800&#160;BC. It is a broken clay tablet that contains a list of <a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_triple" title="Pythagorean triple">Pythagorean triples</a>, that is, integers <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle (a,b,c)}">

        <mi>c</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>

</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7ef0a5a4b8ab98870ae5d6d7c7b4dfe3fb6612e2" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:12.336ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle a^{2}+b^{2}=c^{2}}"></span>. The triples are too numerous and too large to have been obtained by <a href="/wiki/Brute_force_method" class="mw-redirect" title="Brute force method">brute force</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The table's layout suggests that it was constructed by means of what amounts, in modern language, to the <a href="/wiki/Identity_(mathematics)" title="Identity (mathematics)">identity</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-block"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle \left({\frac {1}{2}}\left(x-{\frac {1}{x}}\right)\right)^{2}+1=\left({\frac {1}{2}}\left(x+{\frac {1}{x}}\right)\right)^{2},}">

  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e9be9111eb813cf2c8b31c740ddff47308c1e1a3" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:40.984ex; height:6.509ex;" alt="{\displaystyle \left({\frac {1}{2}}\left(x-{\frac {1}{x}}\right)\right)^{2}+1=\left({\frac {1}{2}}\left(x+{\frac {1}{x}}\right)\right)^{2},}"></span>which is implicit in routine <a href="/wiki/Old_Babylonian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Babylonian language">Old Babylonian</a> exercises.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It has been suggested instead that the table was a source of numerical examples for school problems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEFriberg1981302&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriberg1981302-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Plimpton 322 tablet is the only surviving evidence of what today would be called number theory within Babylonian mathematics, though a kind of <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_mathematics#Algebra" title="Babylonian mathematics">Babylonian algebra</a> was much more developed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEvan&#95;der&#95;Waerden196163-75&#95;26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Waerden196163-75-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Although other civilizations probably influenced Greek mathematics at the beginning,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> all evidence of such borrowings appear relatively late,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-vanderW2&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vanderW2-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-stanencyc&#95;29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanencyc-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and it is likely that Greek <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">arithmētikḗ</i></span> (the theoretical or philosophical study of numbers) is an indigenous tradition.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBoyerMerzbach199182&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyerMerzbach199182-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The ancient Greeks developed a keen interest in <a href="/wiki/Divisibility" class="mw-redirect" title="Divisibility">divisibility</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Pythagoreans" class="mw-redirect" title="Pythagoreans">Pythagoreans</a> attributed mystical quality to <a href="/wiki/Perfect_number" title="Perfect number">perfect</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amicable_numbers" title="Amicable numbers">amicable numbers</a>. The Pythagorean tradition also spoke of so-called <a href="/wiki/Polygonal_number" title="Polygonal number">polygonal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Figurate_numbers" class="mw-redirect" title="Figurate numbers">figurate numbers</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEHeath192176&#95;31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeath192176-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Euclid devoted part of his <i><a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid&#39;s Elements">Elements</a></i> to topics that belong to elementary number theory, including <a href="/wiki/Prime_number" title="Prime number">prime numbers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Divisibility_rule" title="Divisibility rule">divisibility</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He gave the <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_algorithm" title="Euclidean algorithm">Euclidean algorithm</a> for computing the <a href="/wiki/Greatest_common_divisor" title="Greatest common divisor">greatest common divisor</a> of two numbers and a <a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_theorem" title="Euclid&#39;s theorem">proof implying the infinitude of primes</a>. The foremost authority in <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">arithmētikḗ</i></span> in Late Antiquity was <a href="/wiki/Diophantus_of_Alexandria" class="mw-redirect" title="Diophantus of Alexandria">Diophantus of Alexandria</a>, who probably lived in the 3rd century&#160;AD. He wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Arithmetica" class="mw-redirect" title="Arithmetica">Arithmetica</a></i>, a collection of worked-out problems where the task is invariably to find rational solutions to a system of polynomial equations, usually of the form <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle f(x,y)=z^{2}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8d3d49febe8c5fb88fb25d4376433469c13bdbc4" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:11.85ex; height:3.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle f(x,y)=z^{2}}"></span> or <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle f(x,y,z)=w^{2}}">

</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1004a1f588de9d793e07ee911e4378555096b606" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:14.546ex; height:3.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle f(x,y,z)=w^{2}}"></span>. In modern parlance, <a href="/wiki/Diophantine_equation" title="Diophantine equation">Diophantine equations</a> are <a href="/wiki/Polynomial_equation" class="mw-redirect" title="Polynomial equation">polynomial equations</a> to which rational or integer solutions are sought.
</p><p>After the fall of Rome, development shifted to Asia, albeit intermittently. The <a href="/wiki/Chinese_remainder_theorem" title="Chinese remainder theorem">Chinese remainder theorem</a> appears as an exercise<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in <i><a href="/wiki/Sunzi_Suanjing" title="Sunzi Suanjing">Sunzi Suanjing</a></i> (between the third and fifth centuries).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-YongSe&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YongSe-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The result was later generalized with a complete solution called <i>Da-yan-shu</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">大衍術</span></span>) in <a href="/wiki/Qin_Jiushao" title="Qin Jiushao">Qin Jiushao</a>'s 1247 <i><a href="/wiki/Mathematical_Treatise_in_Nine_Sections" title="Mathematical Treatise in Nine Sections">Mathematical Treatise in Nine Sections</a>.</i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There is also some numerical mysticism in Chinese mathematics,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but, unlike that of the Pythagoreans, it seems to have led nowhere. While Greek astronomy probably influenced Indian learning<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPlofker2008119&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlofker2008119-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> it seems to be the case that Indian mathematics is otherwise an autochthonous tradition.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Plofbab&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plofbab-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMumford2010387&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMumford2010387-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/%C4%80ryabha%E1%B9%ADa" class="mw-redirect" title="Āryabhaṭa">Āryabhaṭa</a> (476–550&#160;AD) showed that pairs of simultaneous congruences <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle n\equiv a_{1}{\bmod {m}}_{1}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

        <mi>n</mi>

    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle n\equiv a_{1}{\bmod {m}}_{1}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c663611bcfb301bc7826f1b93ccd106dec042744" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:15.553ex; height:2.509ex;" alt="{\displaystyle n\equiv a_{1}{\bmod {m}}_{1}}"></span>, <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle n\equiv a_{2}{\bmod {m}}_{2}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>n</mi>
        <mo>&#x2261;<!-- ≡ --></mo>
        <msub>
          <mi>a</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <msub>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mo lspace="thickmathspace" rspace="thickmathspace">mod</mo>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi>m</mi>
            </mrow>
          </mrow>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle n\equiv a_{2}{\bmod {m}}_{2}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/70c47219ea418a20f473f14a09b48959cbe71125" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:15.553ex; height:2.509ex;" alt="{\displaystyle n\equiv a_{2}{\bmod {m}}_{2}}"></span> could be solved by a method he called <i>kuṭṭaka</i>, or <i>pulveriser</i>;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> this is a procedure close to the Euclidean algorithm.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMumford2010388&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMumford2010388-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Āryabhaṭa seems to have had in mind applications to astronomical calculations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPlofker2008119&#95;38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlofker2008119-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Brahmagupta (628&#160;AD) started the systematic study of indefinite quadratic equations—in particular, the <a href="/wiki/Pell_equation" class="mw-redirect" title="Pell equation">Pell equation</a>. A general procedure for solving Pell's equation was probably found by <a href="/wiki/Jayadeva_(mathematician)" title="Jayadeva (mathematician)">Jayadeva</a>; the earliest surviving exposition appears in <a href="/wiki/Bh%C4%81skara_II" title="Bhāskara II">Bhāskara II</a>'s Bīja-gaṇita (twelfth century).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPlofker2008194&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlofker2008194-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the early ninth century, the caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma&#39;mun">al-Ma'mun</a> ordered translations of many Greek mathematical works and at least one Sanskrit work.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Plofnot&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plofnot-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Diophantus's main work, the <i>Arithmetica</i>, was translated into Arabic by <a href="/wiki/Qusta_ibn_Luqa" title="Qusta ibn Luqa">Qusta ibn Luqa</a> (820–912).
Part of the treatise <i>al-Fakhri</i> (by <a href="/wiki/Al-Karaj%C4%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Karajī">al-Karajī</a>, 953&#160;– c.&#160;1029) builds on it to some extent. According to Rashed Roshdi, Al-Karajī's contemporary <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham" title="Ibn al-Haytham">Ibn al-Haytham</a> knew<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERashed1980305–321&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERashed1980305–321-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> what would later be called <a href="/wiki/Wilson%27s_theorem" title="Wilson&#39;s theorem">Wilson's theorem</a>. Other than a treatise on squares in arithmetic progression by <a href="/wiki/Fibonacci" title="Fibonacci">Fibonacci</a> no number theory to speak of was done in western Europe during the Middle Ages. Matters started to change in Europe in the late <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, thanks to a renewed study of the works of Greek antiquity. A catalyst was the textual emendation and translation into Latin of Diophantus' <i>Arithmetica</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Pierre_de_Fermat.png" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Portrait of Pierre de Fermat." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Pierre_de_Fermat.png/250px-Pierre_de_Fermat.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="354" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Pierre_de_Fermat.png/330px-Pierre_de_Fermat.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Pierre_de_Fermat.png/500px-Pierre_de_Fermat.png 2x" data-file-width="566" data-file-height="1003" /></a><figcaption>Fermat is sometimes considered the founder of modern number theory.</figcaption></figure>
<p>French mathematician <a href="/wiki/Pierre_de_Fermat" title="Pierre de Fermat">Pierre de Fermat</a> (1607–1665) never published his writings but communicated through correspondence and wrote in marginal notes instead.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWeil198445–46&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeil198445–46-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> His contributions to number theory brought renewed interest in the field in Europe. He conjectured <a href="/wiki/Fermat%27s_little_theorem" title="Fermat&#39;s little theorem">Fermat's little theorem</a>, a basic result in modular arithmetic, and <a href="/wiki/Fermat%27s_Last_Theorem" title="Fermat&#39;s Last Theorem">Fermat's Last Theorem</a>, as well as proved <a href="/wiki/Fermat%27s_right_triangle_theorem" title="Fermat&#39;s right triangle theorem">Fermat's right triangle theorem</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:7&#95;2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He also studied prime numbers, the <a href="/wiki/Four-square_theorem" class="mw-redirect" title="Four-square theorem">four-square theorem</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pell%27s_equations" class="mw-redirect" title="Pell&#39;s equations">Pell's equations</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWeil198492&#95;51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeil198492-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The interest of <a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a> (1707–1783) in number theory was first spurred in 1729, when a friend of his, the amateur<sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christian_Goldbach" title="Christian Goldbach">Christian Goldbach</a>, pointed him towards some of Fermat's work on the subject.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWeil19842,&#95;172&#95;53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeil19842,_172-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEVaradarajan20069&#95;54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaradarajan20069-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This has been called the "rebirth" of modern number theory,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWeil19841–2&#95;55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeil19841–2-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> after Fermat's relative lack of success in getting his contemporaries' attention for the subject.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He proved Fermat's assertions, including <a href="/wiki/Fermat%27s_little_theorem" title="Fermat&#39;s little theorem">Fermat's little theorem</a>; made initial work towards a proof that every integer is the sum of four squares;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWeil1984178–179&#95;57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeil1984178–179-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and specific cases of Fermat's Last Theorem.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He wrote on the link between <a href="/wiki/Simple_continued_fraction" title="Simple continued fraction">continued fractions</a> and Pell's equation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Eulpell&#95;59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eulpell-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWeil1984183&#95;60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeil1984183-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He made the first steps towards analytic number theory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Three European contemporaries continued the work in elementary number theory. <a href="/wiki/Joseph-Louis_Lagrange" title="Joseph-Louis Lagrange">Joseph-Louis Lagrange</a> (1736–1813) gave full proofs of the <a href="/wiki/Four-square_theorem" class="mw-redirect" title="Four-square theorem">four-square theorem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilson%27s_theorem" title="Wilson&#39;s theorem">Wilson's theorem</a>, and developed the basic theory of Pell's equations. <a href="/wiki/Adrien-Marie_Legendre" title="Adrien-Marie Legendre">Adrien-Marie Legendre</a> (1752–1833) stated the <a href="/wiki/Quadratic_reciprocity" title="Quadratic reciprocity">law of quadratic reciprocity.</a> He also conjectured what amounts to the <a href="/wiki/Prime_number_theorem" title="Prime number theorem">prime number theorem</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dirichlet%27s_theorem_on_arithmetic_progressions" title="Dirichlet&#39;s theorem on arithmetic progressions">Dirichlet's theorem on arithmetic progressions</a>. He gave a full treatment of the equation <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle ax^{2}+by^{2}+cz^{2}=0}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

        <msup>
          <mi>x</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>

        <mo>+</mo>
        <mi>b</mi>
        <msup>
          <mi>y</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

        <mi>c</mi>
        <msup>
          <mi>z</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mn>0</mn>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle ax^{2}+by^{2}+cz^{2}=0}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6e7c75ed5d8d24057cd1160d2dfc659972e903af" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:19.919ex; height:3.009ex;" alt="{\displaystyle ax^{2}+by^{2}+cz^{2}=0}"></span>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWeil1984327–328&#95;62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeil1984327–328-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In his old age, he was the first to prove Fermat's Last Theorem for <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle n=5}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>n</mi>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mn>5</mn>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle n=5}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cdb41e9a10a8fd7179b9170149a8d70949ba5d03" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.656ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle n=5}"></span>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWeil1984337–338&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeil1984337–338-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a> (1777–1855) wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Disquisitiones_Arithmeticae" title="Disquisitiones Arithmeticae">Disquisitiones Arithmeticae</a></i> (1801), which had an immense influence in the area of number theory and set its agenda for much of the 19th century. Gauss proved in this work the law of <a href="/wiki/Quadratic_reciprocity" title="Quadratic reciprocity">quadratic reciprocity</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWeil1984332–334&#95;64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeil1984332–334-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and developed the theory of quadratic forms. He also introduced some basic notation to <a href="/wiki/Congruences" class="mw-redirect" title="Congruences">congruences</a> and devoted a section to computational matters, including primality tests.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGoldsteinSchappacher200714&#95;65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldsteinSchappacher200714-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He established a link between <a href="/wiki/Roots_of_unity" class="mw-redirect" title="Roots of unity">roots of unity</a> and number theory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In this way, Gauss arguably made forays towards <a href="/wiki/%C3%89variste_Galois" title="Évariste Galois">Évariste Galois</a>'s work and the area <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_number_theory" title="Algebraic number theory">algebraic number theory</a>.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Georg_Friedrich_Bernhard_Riemann.jpeg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Photograph of Bernhard Reimann." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Georg_Friedrich_Bernhard_Riemann.jpeg/250px-Georg_Friedrich_Bernhard_Riemann.jpeg" decoding="async" width="250" height="273" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Georg_Friedrich_Bernhard_Riemann.jpeg/500px-Georg_Friedrich_Bernhard_Riemann.jpeg 1.5x" data-file-width="903" data-file-height="986" /></a><figcaption>The Riemann hypothesis is of interest in analytic number theory.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Starting early in the nineteenth century, the following developments gradually took place:
</p>
<ul><li>The rise to self-consciousness of number theory (or <i>higher arithmetic</i>) as a field of study.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>The development of much of modern mathematics necessary for basic modern number theory: <a href="/wiki/Complex_analysis" title="Complex analysis">complex analysis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Group_theory" title="Group theory">group theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galois_theory" title="Galois theory">Galois theory</a>—accompanied by greater rigor in analysis and abstraction in algebra.</li>
<li>The rough subdivision of number theory into its modern subfields—in particular, <a href="/wiki/Analytic_number_theory" title="Analytic number theory">analytic</a> and algebraic number theory.</li></ul>
<p>Algebraic number theory may be said to start with the study of reciprocity and <a href="/wiki/Cyclotomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyclotomy">cyclotomy</a>, but truly came into its own with the development of <a href="/wiki/Abstract_algebra" title="Abstract algebra">abstract algebra</a> and early ideal theory and <a href="/wiki/Valuation_(algebra)" title="Valuation (algebra)">valuation</a> theory; see below. A conventional starting point for analytic number theory is <a href="/wiki/Dirichlet%27s_theorem_on_arithmetic_progressions" title="Dirichlet&#39;s theorem on arithmetic progressions">Dirichlet's theorem on arithmetic progressions</a> (1837),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEApostol19767&#95;68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEApostol19767-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEDavenportMontgomery20001&#95;69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavenportMontgomery20001-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> whose proof introduced <a href="/wiki/L-functions" class="mw-redirect" title="L-functions">L-functions</a> and involved some asymptotic analysis and a limiting process on a real variable.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The first use of analytic ideas in number theory actually goes back to Euler (1730s),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEIwaniecKowalski20041&#95;71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIwaniecKowalski20041-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEVaradarajan2006sections&#95;2.5,&#95;3.1&#95;and&#95;6.1&#95;72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaradarajan2006sections_2.5,_3.1_and_6.1-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> who used formal power series and non-rigorous (or implicit) limiting arguments. The use of <i>complex</i> analysis in number theory comes later: the work of <a href="/wiki/Bernhard_Riemann" title="Bernhard Riemann">Bernhard Riemann</a> (1859) on the <a href="/wiki/Riemann_zeta_function" title="Riemann zeta function">zeta function</a> is the canonical starting point;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGranville2008322–348&#95;73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGranville2008322–348-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jacobi%27s_four-square_theorem" title="Jacobi&#39;s four-square theorem">Jacobi's four-square theorem</a> (1839), which predates it, belongs to an initially different strand that has by now taken a leading role in analytic number theory (<a href="/wiki/Modular_form" title="Modular form">modular forms</a>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/American_Mathematical_Society" title="American Mathematical Society">American Mathematical Society</a> awards the <i><a href="/wiki/Cole_Prize" title="Cole Prize">Cole Prize</a> in Number Theory</i>. Moreover, number theory is one of the three mathematical subdisciplines rewarded by the <i><a href="/wiki/Fermat_Prize" title="Fermat Prize">Fermat Prize</a></i>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Main_subdivisions">Main subdivisions</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Number_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Main subdivisions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Elementary_number_theory">Elementary number theory</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Number_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Elementary number theory"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Paul_Erdos_with_Terence_Tao.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Paul Erdős (left) teaching a young Terence Tao (right)." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Paul_Erdos_with_Terence_Tao.jpg/330px-Paul_Erdos_with_Terence_Tao.jpg" decoding="async" width="310" height="209" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Paul_Erdos_with_Terence_Tao.jpg/500px-Paul_Erdos_with_Terence_Tao.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c4/Paul_Erdos_with_Terence_Tao.jpg 2x" data-file-width="515" data-file-height="348" /></a><figcaption>Number theorists <a href="/wiki/Paul_Erd%C5%91s" title="Paul Erdős">Paul Erdős</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terence_Tao" title="Terence Tao">Terence Tao</a> in 1985, when Erdős was 72 and Tao was 10</figcaption></figure>
<p>Elementary number theory deals with the topics in number theory by means of basic methods in arithmetic.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Its primary subjects of study are <a href="/wiki/Divisibility" class="mw-redirect" title="Divisibility">divisibility</a>, <a href="/wiki/Factorization" title="Factorization">factorization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Primality" class="mw-redirect" title="Primality">primality</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Modular_arithmetic" title="Modular arithmetic">congruences</a> in <a href="/wiki/Modular_arithmetic" title="Modular arithmetic">modular arithmetic</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other topics in elementary number theory include <a href="/wiki/Diophantine_equation" title="Diophantine equation">Diophantine equations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Continued_fractions" class="mw-redirect" title="Continued fractions">continued fractions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Integer_partition" title="Integer partition">integer partitions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Diophantine_approximation" title="Diophantine approximation">Diophantine approximations</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Arithmetic is the study of numerical operations and investigates how numbers are combined and transformed using the arithmetic operations of <a href="/wiki/Addition" title="Addition">addition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subtraction" title="Subtraction">subtraction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Multiplication" title="Multiplication">multiplication</a>, <a href="/wiki/Division_(mathematics)" title="Division (mathematics)">division</a>, <a href="/wiki/Exponentiation" title="Exponentiation">exponentiation</a>, extraction of <a href="/wiki/Nth_root" title="Nth root">roots</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logarithm" title="Logarithm">logarithms</a>. Multiplication, for instance, is an operation that combines two numbers, referred to as factors, to form a single number, termed the <a href="/wiki/Product_(mathematics)" title="Product (mathematics)">product</a>, such as <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle 2\times 3=6}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mn>2</mn>
        <mo>&#x00D7;<!-- × --></mo>
        <mn>3</mn>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mn>6</mn>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle 2\times 3=6}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4b249223f0c54e210f6ceeea7ee975b852460448" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:9.426ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle 2\times 3=6}"></span>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Divisibility is a property between two nonzero integers related to division. An integer <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle a}">

  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ffd2487510aa438433a2579450ab2b3d557e5edc" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.23ex; height:1.676ex;" alt="{\displaystyle a}"></span> is said to be divisible by a nonzero integer <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle b}">

An equivalent formulation is that <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle b}">

Conversely, if this were not the case, then <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle a}">

Elementary number theory studies <a href="/wiki/Divisibility_rules" class="mw-redirect" title="Divisibility rules">divisibility rules</a> in order to quickly identify if a given integer is divisible by a fixed divisor. For instance, it is known that any integer is divisible by 3 if its decimal <a href="/wiki/Digit_sum" title="Digit sum">digit sum</a> is divisible by 3.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Richmond-Richmond-2009&#95;78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richmond-Richmond-2009-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Continued_fraction_sqrt3.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="√3 = 1 + 1/(1 + 1/(2 + 1/(1 + 1/(2 + 1/...))))" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5c/Continued_fraction_sqrt3.svg/250px-Continued_fraction_sqrt3.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="138" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5c/Continued_fraction_sqrt3.svg/500px-Continued_fraction_sqrt3.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="476" data-file-height="263" /></a><figcaption>Example of a continued fraction.</figcaption></figure>
<p>A common divisor of several nonzero integers is an integer that divides all of them. The <a href="/wiki/Greatest_common_divisor" title="Greatest common divisor">greatest common divisor</a> (gcd) is the largest of such divisors. Two integers are said to be coprime or relatively prime to one another if their greatest common divisor, and simultaneously their only divisor, is 1. The <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_algorithm" title="Euclidean algorithm">Euclidean algorithm</a> computes the greatest common divisor of two integers <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle a,b}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>a</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi>b</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle a,b}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/181523deba732fda302fd176275a0739121d3bc8" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.261ex; height:2.509ex;" alt="{\displaystyle a,b}"></span> by means of repeatedly applying the division lemma and shifting the divisor and remainder after every step. The algorithm <a href="/wiki/Extended_Euclidean_algorithm" title="Extended Euclidean algorithm">can be extended</a> to solve a special case of <a href="/wiki/Linear_Diophantine_equation" class="mw-redirect" title="Linear Diophantine equation">linear Diophantine equations</a> <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle ax+by=1}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>a</mi>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo>+</mo>
        <mi>b</mi>
        <mi>y</mi>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mn>1</mn>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle ax+by=1}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9c89e287ba09678f910479a16371fbb0da242b6b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:11.814ex; height:2.509ex;" alt="{\displaystyle ax+by=1}"></span>. A Diophantine equation has several unknowns and integer coefficients. Another kind of Diophantine equation is described in the <a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_theorem" title="Pythagorean theorem">Pythagorean theorem</a>, <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle x^{2}+y^{2}=z^{2}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

          </mrow>
        </msup>
        <mo>+</mo>
        <msup>
          <mi>y</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <msup>
          <mi>z</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x^{2}+y^{2}=z^{2}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/24defb565dbcba7850e1bfb51176bcf574b4e56b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:12.682ex; height:3.009ex;" alt="{\displaystyle x^{2}+y^{2}=z^{2}}"></span>, whose solutions are called Pythagorean triples if they are all integers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:6&#95;11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another kind of expression is the <a href="/wiki/Continued_fraction" title="Continued fraction">continued fraction</a>, which writes a sum of an integer and a fraction whose denominator is another such sum.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Elementary number theory studies the divisibility properties of integers such as <a href="/wiki/Parity_(mathematics)" title="Parity (mathematics)">parity</a> (even and odd numbers), <a href="/wiki/Prime_numbers" class="mw-redirect" title="Prime numbers">prime numbers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Perfect_numbers" class="mw-redirect" title="Perfect numbers">perfect numbers</a>. Important number-theoretic functions include the <a href="/wiki/Divisor_function" title="Divisor function">divisor-counting function</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Divisor_summatory_function" title="Divisor summatory function">divisor summatory function</a> and its modifications, and <a href="/wiki/Euler%27s_totient_function" title="Euler&#39;s totient function">Euler's totient function</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Prime_number" title="Prime number">prime number</a> is an integer greater than 1 whose only positive divisors are 1 and the prime itself. A positive integer greater than 1 that is not prime is called a composite number. <a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_theorem" title="Euclid&#39;s theorem">Euclid's theorem</a> demonstrates that there are infinitely many prime numbers that comprise the set <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle \{2,3,5,7,11,\cdots \}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">{</mo>
        <mn>2</mn>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mn>3</mn>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mn>5</mn>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mn>7</mn>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mn>11</mn>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mo>&#x22EF;<!-- ⋯ --></mo>
        <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">}</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \{2,3,5,7,11,\cdots \}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9e638ceab8869195667e947f0112e4553be0b5cf" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:17.193ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle \{2,3,5,7,11,\cdots \}}"></span>. The <a href="/wiki/Sieve_of_Eratosthenes" title="Sieve of Eratosthenes">sieve of Eratosthenes</a> was devised as an efficient algorithm for identifying all primes up to a given natural number by eliminating all composite numbers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Factorization" title="Factorization">Factorization</a> is a method of expressing a number as a <a href="/wiki/Product_(mathematics)" title="Product (mathematics)">product</a>. Specifically in number theory, <a href="/wiki/Integer_factorization" title="Integer factorization">integer factorization</a> is the decomposition of an integer into a product of integers. The process of repeatedly applying this procedure until all factors are prime is known as <a href="/wiki/Prime_factorization" class="mw-redirect" title="Prime factorization">prime factorization</a>. A fundamental property of primes is shown in <a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_lemma" title="Euclid&#39;s lemma">Euclid's lemma</a>. It is a consequence of the lemma that if a prime divides a product of integers, then that prime divides at least one of the factors in the product. The <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_arithmetic" title="Fundamental theorem of arithmetic">unique factorization theorem</a> is the fundamental theorem of arithmetic that relates to prime factorization. The theorem states that every integer greater than 1 can be factorised into a product of prime numbers and that this factorisation is unique up to the order of the factors. For example, <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle 120}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

      </mstyle>

</p><p><a href="/wiki/Modular_arithmetic" title="Modular arithmetic">Modular arithmetic</a> works with finite sets of integers and introduces the concepts of congruence and residue classes. A congruence of two integers <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle a,b}">

    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\textstyle a^{p-1}\equiv 1{\pmod {p}}}</annotation>

</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4fdd1b1dc19bb86e04f905f5ba03e2d1862ef071" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:18.214ex; height:3.009ex;" alt="{\textstyle a^{p-1}\equiv 1{\pmod {p}}}"></span> is true. <a href="/wiki/Euler%27s_theorem" title="Euler&#39;s theorem">Euler's theorem</a> extends this to assert that every integer <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle n}">

Modular arithmetic also provides formulas that are used to solve congruences with unknowns in a similar vein to equation solving in algebra, such as the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_remainder_theorem" title="Chinese remainder theorem">Chinese remainder theorem</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Analytic_number_theory">Analytic number theory</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Number_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Analytic number theory"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Analytic_number_theory" title="Analytic number theory">Analytic number theory</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Complex_zeta.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Complex_zeta.jpg/250px-Complex_zeta.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="250" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Complex_zeta.jpg/500px-Complex_zeta.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="821" data-file-height="820" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Riemann_zeta_function" title="Riemann zeta function">Riemann zeta function</a> ζ(<i>s</i>) in the <a href="/wiki/Complex_plane" title="Complex plane">complex plane</a>. The color of a point <i>s</i> gives the value of ζ(<i>s</i>): dark colors denote values close to zero and hue gives the value's <a href="/wiki/Argument_(complex_analysis)" title="Argument (complex analysis)">argument</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:ModularGroup-FundamentalDomain.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/ModularGroup-FundamentalDomain.svg/250px-ModularGroup-FundamentalDomain.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="113" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/ModularGroup-FundamentalDomain.svg/500px-ModularGroup-FundamentalDomain.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="401" data-file-height="181" /></a><figcaption>The action of the <a href="/wiki/Modular_group" title="Modular group">modular group</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Upper_half_plane" class="mw-redirect" title="Upper half plane">upper half plane</a>. The region in grey is the standard <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_domain" title="Fundamental domain">fundamental domain</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Analytic number theory, in contrast to elementary number theory, relies on <a href="/wiki/Complex_numbers" class="mw-redirect" title="Complex numbers">complex numbers</a> and techniques from analysis and calculus. Analytic number theory may be defined
</p>
<ul><li>in terms of its tools, as the study of the integers by means of tools from <a href="/wiki/Real_analysis" title="Real analysis">real</a> and <a href="/wiki/Complex_analysis" title="Complex analysis">complex</a> analysis;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEApostol19767&#95;68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEApostol19767-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or</li>
<li>in terms of its concerns, as the study within number theory of estimates on the size and density of certain numbers (e.g., primes), as opposed to identities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<p>It studies the distribution of primes, behavior of number-theoretic functions, and irrational numbers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Number theory has the reputation of being a field many of whose results can be stated to the layperson. At the same time, many of the proofs of these results are not particularly accessible, in part because the range of tools they use is, if anything, unusually broad within mathematics.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The following are examples of problems in analytic number theory: the <a href="/wiki/Prime_number_theorem" title="Prime number theorem">prime number theorem</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Goldbach_conjecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Goldbach conjecture">Goldbach conjecture</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Twin_prime_conjecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Twin prime conjecture">twin prime conjecture</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hardy%E2%80%93Littlewood_conjecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Hardy–Littlewood conjecture">Hardy–Littlewood conjectures</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Waring_problem" class="mw-redirect" title="Waring problem">Waring problem</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Riemann_hypothesis" title="Riemann hypothesis">Riemann hypothesis</a>. Some of the most important tools of analytic number theory are the <a href="/wiki/Circle_method" class="mw-redirect" title="Circle method">circle method</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sieve_theory" title="Sieve theory">sieve methods</a> and <a href="/wiki/L-functions" class="mw-redirect" title="L-functions">L-functions</a> (or, rather, the study of their properties). The theory of <a href="/wiki/Modular_form" title="Modular form">modular forms</a> (and, more generally, <a href="/wiki/Automorphic_forms" class="mw-redirect" title="Automorphic forms">automorphic forms</a>) also occupies an increasingly central place in the toolbox of analytic number theory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mathematical_analysis" title="Mathematical analysis">Analysis</a> is the branch of mathematics that studies the <a href="/wiki/Limit_(mathematics)" title="Limit (mathematics)">limit</a>, defined as the value to which a sequence or function tends as the argument (or index) approaches a specific value. For example, the limit of the sequence 0.9, 0.99, 0.999, ... is 1. In the context of functions, the limit of <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\textstyle {\frac {1}{x}}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mfrac>

            <mi>x</mi>
          </mfrac>

Every complex number can be expressed as <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle x+iy}">

</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Distribution_of_primes" class="mw-redirect" title="Distribution of primes">distribution of primes</a>, described by the function <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle \pi }">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>&#x03C0;<!-- π --></mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \pi }</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9be4ba0bb8df3af72e90a0535fabcc17431e540a" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.332ex; height:1.676ex;" alt="{\displaystyle \pi }"></span> that counts all primes up to a given real number, is unpredictable and is a major subject of study in number theory. Elementary formulas for a partial sequence of primes, including <a href="/wiki/Lucky_numbers_of_Euler" title="Lucky numbers of Euler">Euler's prime-generating polynomials</a> have been developed. However, these cease to function as the primes become too large. The prime number theorem in analytic number theory provides a formalisation of the notion that prime numbers appear less commonly as their numerical value increases. One distribution states, informally, that the function <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {x}{\log(x)}}}">

            </mrow>

Another distribution involves an offset logarithmic integral which converges to <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle \pi (x)}">

</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5a62d9bf7b2ba03b30a95e7059362cc4b3865123" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.471ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle \pi (x)}"></span> more quickly.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:5&#95;3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Riemann_Explicit_Formula.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Riemann_Explicit_Formula.gif/250px-Riemann_Explicit_Formula.gif" decoding="async" width="250" height="123" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Riemann_Explicit_Formula.gif/500px-Riemann_Explicit_Formula.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="781" data-file-height="383" /></a><figcaption>Corrections to an <a href="/wiki/Prime-counting_function#Exact_form" title="Prime-counting function">estimate</a> of the prime-counting function using zeros of the zeta function</figcaption></figure>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Zeta_function" class="mw-redirect" title="Zeta function">zeta function</a> has been demonstrated to be connected to the distribution of primes. It is defined as the series<span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-block"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle \zeta (s)=\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }{\frac {1}{n^{s}}}={\frac {1}{1^{s}}}+{\frac {1}{2^{s}}}+{\frac {1}{3^{s}}}+\cdots }">

        <mi>s</mi>

    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle s}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/01d131dfd7673938b947072a13a9744fe997e632" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.09ex; height:1.676ex;" alt="{\displaystyle s}"></span> is greater than 1. Euler demonstrated a link involving the infinite product over all prime numbers, expressed as the identity <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-block"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle \zeta (s)=\prod _{p{\text{ prime}}}\left(1-{\frac {1}{p^{s}}}\right)^{-1}.}">

  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/721e49255ab6b9adc934989b577c2decbd7578bb" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -3.338ex; width:26.415ex; height:7.343ex;" alt="{\displaystyle \zeta (s)=\prod _{p{\text{ prime}}}\left(1-{\frac {1}{p^{s}}}\right)^{-1}.}"></span>Riemann extended the definition to a complex variable and conjectured that all nontrivial cases (<span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle 0&lt;\Re (s)&lt;1}">

    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

        <mo>&lt;</mo>

        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mo>&lt;</mo>
        <mn>1</mn>
      </mstyle>

    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle 0&lt;\Re (s)&lt;1}</annotation>
